        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="2d908412208349a49054631c29b028e3"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2d908412208349a49054631c29b028e3">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '67692489-3aea-4c33-b72d-3fd3221a27e0'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d58303647" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Preventing the aggregation of nanosized electrode materials is a key point to fully utilize the advantage of the high capacity. In this work, a facile and low-cost surface solvation treatment is developed to synthesize Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticles, which efficiently prevents the aggregation of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> primary nanoparticles. The reaction between alcohol molecules and surface hydroxy groups is confirmed by density functional theory calculations and Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy. The electrochemical mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> as lithium-ion battery anode is characterized by in situ X-ray diffraction for the first time. This electrode material is capable of delivering a high reversible discharge capacity of 799 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a high initial coulombic efficiency of 79%, and the capacity retention is 78% after 500 cycles. Moreover, a remarkable reversible discharge capacity of 679 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, when tested as sodium-ion battery anode, a high reversible capacity of 382 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be delivered at the current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which still retains at 229 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. The superior electrochemical performance makes it a potential anode material for high-rate and long-life lithium/sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201804706-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201804706-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Currently, graphite is still the predominant commercial anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), which has been proven successful in the electric vehicles market.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0001_R_d58303638e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0002_R_d58303638e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Small">2</a></span> However, the existing LIBs have encountered the bottleneck as graphite is reaching its theoretical limit (372 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) and suffering from the poor rate performance, as well as the limitation and asymmetrical distribution of lithium resource.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0003_R_d58303638e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">3</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0004_R_d58303638e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a></span> On the other hand, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attention due to its abundant sodium resource, low cost, and the similar electrochemical reaction to LIBs, which is considered as the promising battery technology for large-scale energy storage applications.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0005_R_d58303638e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Small">5</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0006_R_d58303638e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy">6</a></span> But the commercial graphite does not work for SIBs as its interlayers are incapable to accommodate the sodium ions with larger ionic size.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0007_R_d58303638e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a></span> As a result, one promising step to make the technological advancements is to develop the novel anode materials with both lithium and sodium storage ability.</p>
         
         <p>In this regard, transition metal oxides (M<i><sub>a</sub></i>O<i><sub>b</sub></i>, M = Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, etc.) based on the conversion reaction mechanism have been widely explored as the potential anode materials for LIBs and SIBs. Compared to graphite, transition metal oxides can provide higher theoretical specific capacity and higher safety, because the higher reaction potential can eliminate the lithium/sodium dendrite problem.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0008_R_d58303638e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Today">8</a>-<a href="#smll201804706-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0015_R_d58303638e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small">15</a></span> As an important member in the transition metal oxide family, transition metal vanadate has attracted much attention due to the multivalent properties of vanadium, which could deliver considerable capacity based on the multielectron transfer.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0016_R_d58303638e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0017_R_d58303638e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochim. Acta">17</a></span> Additionally, the vanadium oxide presents strong V<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond during electrochemical process, suggesting the smaller volume change compared with traditional conversion-type electrodes.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0018_R_d58303638e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></span> For LIBs application, it is reported that the vanadium oxide can store lithium ions on the basis of the intercalation reaction mechanism, and provide the reaction sites for the conversion reaction between transition metal and metal oxide, preventing the aggregation of the obtained highly active metal nanograins, improving the cycling performance of the active material.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0019_R_d58303638e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0020_R_d58303638e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></span> Furthermore, the binary metal oxide could exhibit higher electrochemical activity compared to the single metal oxide electrode.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0021_R_d58303638e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Environ. Sci.">21</a></span> Calcium vanadate nanowires have been synthesized as a novel sodium-ion anode material, which delivered a superior cycling performance (1600 cycles), excellent rate performance (5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and an applicable reversible capacity (&gt;300 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0016_R_d58303638e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></span> Lou's group has developed triple-shelled Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@Co<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub> nanoboxes as electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, which retain a high capacity of 948 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0022_R_d58303638e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></span></p>
         
         <p>However, as one important kind of vanadates, iron vanadates with high abundance and environmental friendliness have been rarely studied as lithium/sodium-ion anode materials.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0023_R_d58303638e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a>-<a href="#smll201804706-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0028_R_d58303638e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Lett.">28</a></span> To our best knowledge, the first research on the iron vanadates as lithium-ion battery anode can be traced back to Xia's work in 2009. Solid-state method was applied to synthesize spinel Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> with the average size of 100 nm. However, the severe aggregation of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles was observed, and the specific discharge capacity was only 225 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0028_R_d58303638e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Lett.">28</a></span> In order to fully realize the high theoretical capacity of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>, the structure should be delicately designed. Nanostructure can provide not only a reduced diffusion length for lithium/sodium mitigation,<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0002_R_d58303638e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Small">2</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0022_R_d58303638e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0029_R_d58303638e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">29</a>-<a href="#smll201804706-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0031_R_d58303638e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></span> but also an interdiffusion interface between M<sup>0</sup> and Li<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>O, improving the reversibility of active materials.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0032_R_d58303638e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Environ. Sci.">32</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0033_R_d58303638e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nano Energy">33</a></span> However, it is worth noting that nanostructured electrode would exhibit increased side reaction with electrolyte decomposition, severe aggregation issue during the electrochemical process, and the low tap density, which would be obstacles for the application.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0034_R_d58303638e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a></span> To tackle the challenges mentioned above, a stable 3D hierarchical porous microstructure composed of primary nanoparticles would be an optimal structure, which may combine the favorable characteristics of both nanostructured and microstructured materials.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0020_R_d58303638e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we synthesized the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticles by a facile surface solvation treatment of the precursor. The experimental work is supported by density functional theory (DFT) calculation and Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectroscopy, which explore the solvation process at the molecular level. In the resulting porous microstructure, the primary nanoparticles can provide greatly reduced pathways for ion/electron transportation and increased interfaces between M<sup>0</sup> and Li<sub>2</sub>O, while the porous structure can offer an open configuration for electrolyte penetration and buffer the volume expansion during cycling. The electrochemical mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> as LIBs anode was characterized by in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) for the first time. When evaluated as anode materials for LIBs, the obtained Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> porous microparticles exhibited a high reversible discharge capacity of 799 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 78% after 500 cycles, and the remarkable reversible discharge capacity of 679 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Meanwhile, when evaluated as SIB anode, a high reversible capacity of 382 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be delivered at the current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which still retained as 229 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. Hence, we believe that the excellent electrochemical performance makes the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticles a promising anode for high-performance LIBs/SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201804706-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201804706-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> precursor nanoparticles with good dispersion and the low crystallinity were prepared through a hydrothermal reaction, and the diameter is less than 50 nm (scanning electron microscopic (SEM) images in Figure S1A,B, Supporting Information). However, after regular washing process, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> dense microparticles precursor (Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP precursor) with low crystallinity degree were obtained, in which the primary nanoparticles are tightly attached to each other (SEM images in Figure S1C,D, Supporting Information, and XRD patterns in Figure S2, Supporting Information). According to the previous reports, the coordinated water molecules and hydroxyl groups on the surface of the nanoparticles synthesized in aqueous solution are the fundamental cause of the formation of hard agglomerates.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0035_R_d58303638e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Sci.">35</a>-<a href="#smll201804706-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0037_R_d58303638e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J Am. Ceram. Soc.">37</a></span> The formation mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP is proposed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0001">1</a>A. During the drying process, the free water molecules surrounding the precursor nanoparticles first evaporate, leading to the formation of hydrogen bonds between terminal hydroxyl groups of the neighboring particles, which would finally transform into the chemical bonds during further dehydration process, yielding hard agglomerates (Mechanism I, Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0001">1</a>A). After calcination, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> dense microparticles (Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP) were obtained, which are unfavorable for the electrochemical performance (SEM and transmission electron microscopic (TEM) images in Figure S3, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201804706-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2148411c-bb9d-4532-88da-d12d19734275/smll201804706-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2148411c-bb9d-4532-88da-d12d19734275/smll201804706-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/72d23ab9-e6f7-4328-add7-ffc61e1fc07a/smll201804706-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2148411c-bb9d-4532-88da-d12d19734275/smll201804706-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201804706-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201804706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) The proposed mechanisms for agglomerate formation without (I) and with (II) surface solvation treatment. B) Structures and calculated binding/dehydration free energies of reaction between alcohol and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-OH surface.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Surface solvation treatment has been proven to be an effective method to eliminate the hard aggregation of nanoparticles.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0036_R_d58303638e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Am. Ceram. Soc.">36</a></span> Based on this idea, we simply introduced a facile surface solvation treatment step after regular washing to optimize the structure, and the mechanism is illustrated as Mechanism II in Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0001">1</a>A. First of all, the regular washed precursor nanoparticles are redispersed in ethanol. During the stirring process, the hydroxyl groups on the particle surface are replaced by ethoxy groups, and no hydrogen bonds would exist between adjacent nanoparticles, which is defined as the solvation step. During the following drying step, ethoxy group would be removed without the formation of the strong chemical bond between neighboring particles, yielding soft agglomerates.</p>
         
         <p>DFT calculations were operated to support the proposed mechanism (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0001">1</a>B). In the beginning, the ethanol could be adsorbed on (−112) surface of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> through the hydrogen bonding with the surface hydroxyl groups, with a negative reaction free energy (Δ<i>G</i><sub>1</sub>) of −0.973 eV. The following step is the reaction between CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH and the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-OH surface to form Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O. The corresponding binding free energy (Δ<i>G</i><sub>2</sub>) is calculated to be −0.155 eV (Table S1, Supporting Information), indicating that the CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH could spontaneously bind to hydroxyl-terminated Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> surface and then dehydrate with the surface hydroxyl group to form Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub> surface, which could effectively reduce the aggregation of primary nanoparticles.</p>
         
         <p>To confirm this hypothesis, the FTIR spectra of the two precursors are compared in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0002">2</a>A. The most significant differences are the existence of the peaks centered at ≈1152 and ≈1212 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are corresponding to the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O stretching, indicating the chemical reaction of ethanol with the hydroxyl groups on the surface of the nanoparticles.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0036_R_d58303638e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Am. Ceram. Soc.">36</a></span> The pH value of the reaction system was further tested, which can clearly reflect the chemistry state of the nanoparticles surface. Consistent with the hypothesis, the pH value of the solution after hydrothermal reaction was 11, and the precipitates showed a negative zeta potential of −24.5 mV, indicating that the primary nanoparticles were covered with abundant hydroxyl groups. Due to the electrostatic repulsion, the nanoparticles exhibited relatively good dispersion and narrow size distribution (Figure S4A, Supporting Information), and no secondary particles were observed at this stage (Figure S1A,B, Supporting Information). After the regular washing process, the negative zeta potential of the obtained nanoparticles increased to −11.8 mV. Due to the existence of hydroxyl groups, the primary nanoparticles grew into the hard agglomerates during the water evaporation process, which exhibited a wide size distribution and high <i>D</i><sub>50</sub> value of 8.996 µm (Figure S4B, Supporting Information). However, after the surface solvation treatment, the negative zeta potential further increased to −0.862 mV, indicating the efficient replacement of hydroxyl groups with ethoxy groups. The obtained soft agglomerates showed a narrow size distribution and low <i>D</i><sub>50</sub> value of 3.626 µm (Figures S4C and S5, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201804706-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/79562f6a-e495-4917-86d8-30705e482a9d/smll201804706-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/79562f6a-e495-4917-86d8-30705e482a9d/smll201804706-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/525529b3-590e-48ab-b034-28719d4f472a/smll201804706-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/79562f6a-e495-4917-86d8-30705e482a9d/smll201804706-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201804706-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201804706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) FTIR patterns of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP precursor and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP precursor. B) XRD pattern of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP. C) SEM, D,E) TEM, and F) high-resolution TEM images of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The XRD patterns of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP both indicate the pure cubic Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> phase (JCPDS No. 01-075-1519) without any impurities,<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0028_R_d58303638e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Mater. Lett.">28</a></span> which means that the surface solvation treatment did not change the final phase of the material (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0002">2</a>B). SEM and TEM images show the hierarchical porous architecture of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP, which is made up of a large quantity of nanoparticles with diameter less than 50 nm, revealing that the mesoporous nanostructure of the precursor can be well persevered after high-temperature calcination (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0002">2</a>C–E). Moreover, the corresponding elemental mapping (EDS) images of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP indicate that oxygen, iron, and vanadium homogeneously distribute within the whole secondary microstructure (Figure S6, Supporting Information). The corresponding selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (inset of Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0002">2</a>D) implies the polycrystalline nature of the as-synthesized Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP, and the diffraction rings can be readily indexed to the (111), (220), (311), and (400) planes of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP. In the high-resolution TEM image in Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0002">2</a>F, lattice fringes with interplane distance of 0.48 and 0.29 nm can be observed, corresponding to the (111) and (220) planes of cubic Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>, respectively. The structure comparison between Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP indicates that the surface solvation treatment plays an important role in the formation of the hierarchical porous microstructure.</p>
         
         <p>The nitrogen adsorption–desorption measurement was also applied to evaluate the mesoporous structure of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP before and after calcination. The nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms of the precursor and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> products are illustrated in Figure S7 (Supporting Information). Both of them exhibit the typical type <i>IV</i> curves, representing the well-developed mesoporous structure formed by the primary particles.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0038_R_d58303638e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Environ. Sci.">38</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0039_R_d58303638e890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Energy">39</a></span> The calculated specific surface area, total pore volume, and average pore size of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP precursor are 146.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.315 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, and 8.70 nm, respectively. However, without the surface solvation treatment, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP precursor presents much less specific surface area, total pore volume, and average pore size of 68.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.0655 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, and 3.82 nm, respectively. After the calcination process, the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area and total pore volume of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP are calculated to be 31.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.118 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are much higher than those of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP (11.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.055 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively). Based on the structure characterization mentioned above, it has become well established that the surface solvation treatment results in higher specific surface area and total pore volume, and more uniform distribution of the particle and pore size.</p>
         
         <p>In order to investigate the lithium storage process, the corresponding cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP were characterized. Both samples display similar redox peaks in the first cycle (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0003">3</a>A). However, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP exhibits larger area, higher redox currents, and less polarization at the same scan rate, which is due to the faster electron/ion transport kinetics than that of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0040_R_d58303638e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a></span> In the first cathodic curve, three peaks around 0.85, 0.59, and 0.39 V are ascribed to the multistep electrochemical reaction. While the peaks at 1.42 and 2.15 V in the charge process correspond to the reversible extraction of lithium ions in the product. The subsequent CV curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP are quite different from the initial one, which is common for the metal oxide anode and usually attributed to the irreversible decomposition of electrolyte and crystal structure transformation during the first cycle (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0003">3</a>B). The subsequent second and third scans exhibit almost overlapped curves, indicating the excellent cycling stability. The cathodic peaks located at 0.79 and 1.75 V can be well associated with the multistep reduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup> to Fe<sup>0</sup>, lithiation of vanadium oxide, as well as the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. While the anodic peaks located at 1.41 and 2.11 V could be assigned to the multistep oxidation of Fe<sup>0</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup> and the delithiation of vanadium oxide. Besides, the CV curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP at the scan rates of 0.1, 0.5, 1, and 2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> are further compared in Figure S8 (Supporting Information). All of the CV curves exhibit two pairs of reduction/oxidation peaks in each cycle, indicating the exceptional rate capability of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201804706-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/034f9c6b-6ecd-4dc5-9091-bcbf5ee0ca5c/smll201804706-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/034f9c6b-6ecd-4dc5-9091-bcbf5ee0ca5c/smll201804706-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1eb2c4c7-b87b-4f6a-83bb-cc089cdea44b/smll201804706-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/034f9c6b-6ecd-4dc5-9091-bcbf5ee0ca5c/smll201804706-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201804706-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201804706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP as anodes for LIBs. A) The first cycle of CV curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP at a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. B) The second and third cycles of CV curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP at a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. C,D) In situ X-ray diffraction patterns of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP during first cycle.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> anode has been rarely explored, the detailed electrochemical reaction mechanism of this material remains unclear. The in situ XRD technique was employed to investigate the reaction mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0003">3</a>C,D). In order to show the characteristic peaks at a wider range, the ex situ XRD results are also exhibited in Figure S9 (Supporting Information). Initially, the peaks at 30.0° and 35.4° can be clearly observed, which is consistent with the original XRD pattern. Along with the lithium-ion intercalation, the peaks shift to the lower angles at 29.7° and 35.1°, indicating the formation of Li<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS No. 00-037-1432), which is a common phenomenon for iron oxide anode material.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0041_R_d58303638e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></span> Meanwhile, a new peak at 35.7° appears around the voltage of 1.1 V, which can be well ascribed to the Li<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>1.25</sub>V<sub>1.25</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS No. 01-074-2358). Based on this, it is obvious that only the lithium intercalation phenomenon can be observed above the voltage of 1.0 V. Then, the newly emerged peaks gradually disappear during the first plateau region, along with the formation of a new peak at 36.6°, which is the characterized peak of FeO (JCPDS No. 00-001-1223). Combining with the ex situ XRD result in Figure S9 (Supporting Information), the CV peak around 0.85 V in the discharge process might be associated with the decomposition of Li<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Li<sub>0.5</sub>Fe<sub>1.25</sub>V<sub>1.25</sub>O<sub>4</sub> into FeO and Li<sub>0.59</sub>VO<sub>2</sub>. Though the in situ and ex situ XRD patterns at 0.5 V are quite similar to that at 0.7 V, the peaks shift to the lower angle. Based on this result, we can conclude that the CV curve around 0.59 V might be attributed to the further lithiation of FeO and Li<sub>0.59</sub>VO<sub>2</sub>. When Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP is further discharged, the peak density decreases gradually and no obvious characteristic peaks can be observed, implying the amorphous state of the final reaction product. Based on this experimental result, the peak located at 0.39 V in the CV curve is ascribed to the conversion formation of metallic Fe, further lithiation of Li<i><sub>x</sub></i>VO<sub>2</sub>, as well as the formation of SEI. While during the charge process, the metallic Fe would transform into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and the lithium ions would extract from the vanadium oxides. However, the obtained ultrasmall Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> products are well dispersed in the amorphous vanadium oxides without a long-range-ordering status, there is no crystallization peak observed in X-ray diffraction pattern (Figure S9, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The rate performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP was evaluated at various current densities (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0004">4</a>A). Consistent with the structure comparison, the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP exhibits better rate capability as well as higher capacities. The second discharge capacities of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP are 793, 721, 641, and 551 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, when decreasing the current density back to 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity quickly increases to 724 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating a good reversibility. While the second discharge capacities of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP are 579, 464, 392, and 263 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When reducing the current density back to 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity increases to 454 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial charge/discharge profiles of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> are compared in Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0004">4</a>B. Both of them exhibit three pronounced plateaus during the discharge process, which are consistent with the CV curves. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted to compare the electrochemical kinetics of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP system (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0004">4</a>C,D). The impedance spectra collected before cycle, after one and ten cycles are compared, as Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP always presents smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> than that of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP (Table S2, Supporting Information). This result reveals that the nanoscale subunits and unique porous structure of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP can provide more accessible pathway for charge transfer.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0042_R_d58303638e1203" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Mater. Chem. A">42</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201804706-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9bb42832-a6b2-40ad-8703-cbbcd7efc75b/smll201804706-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9bb42832-a6b2-40ad-8703-cbbcd7efc75b/smll201804706-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b59ff6ce-9059-4524-8f52-063ffb9f7b47/smll201804706-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9bb42832-a6b2-40ad-8703-cbbcd7efc75b/smll201804706-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201804706-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201804706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP as anodes for LIBs. A) The rate performance of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP at various rates from 0.5 to 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. B) The first charge/discharge curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP at current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The typical Nyquist plots of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP C) before cycling and D) after different cycle times. E) The detailed cycling performance of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP at different current densities.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The long-term cycling performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP were further examined at 0.5, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0004">4</a>E, and Figure S10, Supporting Information). Particularly, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP is capable of delivering high reversible discharge capacities of 799, 717, and 679 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with high initial coulombic efficiencies of 79, 79, and 76%, respectively. It is interesting that the discharge capacities increase gradually after the initial recession, which is a common phenomenon for the metal oxide electrode materials.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0043_R_d58303638e1286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a>-<a href="#smll201804706-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0045_R_d58303638e1286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></span> According to the CV, in situ XRD, and ex situ XRD results, the original Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> transforms into the ultrafine nanocrystalline iron oxide and amorphous lithium vanadium oxide with lower conductivity, leading to the initial capacity decrease. Along with the further cycling, more active materials participate in the electrochemical process, and the discharge capacities increase continuously afterward and finally stabilized at 624, 483, and 393 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles, respectively. While the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-DMP shows much lower discharge capacities at 0.5, 2, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as well as lower initial coulombic efficiencies of 73, 73, and 70%, respectively (Figure S10, Supporting Information). The specific capacities and initial coulombic efficiencies of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP and Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP are compared in Figure S11 (Supporting Information), which clearly demonstrates the superior lithium storage property of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP.</p>
         
         <p>To verify the structure advantage of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP, the morphology evolution of active material after 100 cycles was characterized by ex situ SEM and TEM. According to the SEM results, the microstructure assembled with the primary nanoparticles is well preserved (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0005">5</a>A,B). Moreover, it is clear that the microparticles are well covered with the uniform solid electrolyte interface, which is beneficial for the long-term cycling performance. TEM images of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP anode after 100 cycles demonstrate that some ordered domains (2–5 nm) with regular lattice fringe are located in the amorphous matrix (Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0005">5</a>C–E). According to the previous report, the amorphous matrix is composed of lithiated vanadium oxides.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0020_R_d58303638e1333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></span> During the electrochemical process, they can serve as the reaction sites for iron oxide and prevent the agglomeration of the obtained nanocrystals, thus improve the reversibility of the electrochemical reaction. The individual nanocrystal in Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0005">5</a>F exhibits clear crystal lattices with spacings of 0.20 and 0.21 nm, corresponding to the (041) and (400) planes of cubic Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS No. 00-039-1346). All of the structure characterization suggests that the original Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> has transformed into iron oxide and lithium vanadium oxide during the electrochemical process. The morphology and structure transformation mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP during the electrochemical process as LIBs anode is schematically demonstrated in Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0005">5</a>G.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201804706-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8b85bcc8-25f1-4a0f-b1c7-68eb3f887254/smll201804706-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8b85bcc8-25f1-4a0f-b1c7-68eb3f887254/smll201804706-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/377388b2-27a9-4fe2-9476-3f0af9e22090/smll201804706-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8b85bcc8-25f1-4a0f-b1c7-68eb3f887254/smll201804706-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201804706-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201804706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A,B) Ex situ SEM images of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP collected at the voltage of 2.5 V after 100 cycles; C–F) Ex situ TEM and high-resolution TEM images of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP collected at the voltage of 2.5 V after 100 cycles; G) The morphology and structure transformation mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP during the electrochemical process as LIBs anode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Inspired by the superior lithium storage property and structure stability of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP, the Na-ion storage performances were also investigated in half cells, which have never been explored before. The CV test was operated in the voltage of 0.01–2.5 V at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> to display the electrochemical behavior of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP (Figure S12, Supporting Information). Similar to the CV curves obtained in LIBs test, the first CV curve is different from the following cycles. The cathodic peak located at 0.97 V is associated with the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> and the irreversible decomposition of the original structure. While the cathodic peak located at 0.80 and 0.24 V can be attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into vanadium oxide, the reduction of Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<i><sub>y</sub></i> to Fe<sup>0</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>O, and the formation of SEI layer. While the corresponding anodic peaks located at 0.71 and 1.62 V indicate the desodiation reactions of sodium vanadium oxide and the oxidation of Fe<sup>0</sup> to Fe<i><sub>x</sub></i>O<i><sub>y</sub></i>.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0046_R_d58303638e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Mater. Chem. A">46</a>-<a href="#smll201804706-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0048_R_d58303638e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Mater. Chem. A">48</a></span> The rate capability of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP at current densities ranging from 0.2 to 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> is explored in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0006">6</a>A. The high reversible specific capacities of 601, 460, 404, 368, and 325 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be obtained at current densities of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density decreases to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 365 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> could be recovered. Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0006">6</a>B shows the initial charge/discharge profiles during rate test of the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP electrode for SIBs. It is obvious that the first-cycle discharge/charge plateaus at the current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> are consistent with the initial CV curves. What's more, the high-rate and long-term cycling stability is further investigated at a high current density of 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. According to Figure <a href="#smll201804706-fig-0006">6</a>C, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP electrode delivered a high reversible capacity of 229 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at the end of 1000 cycles. These superior electrochemical properties indicate that Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP can be applied as anode materials in both LIBs and SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201804706-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8bc0f9c-ec9e-48d7-bfc8-5aee62c25017/smll201804706-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8bc0f9c-ec9e-48d7-bfc8-5aee62c25017/smll201804706-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3a5fc99a-3aed-4c37-be4d-dab6f91a6edc/smll201804706-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8bc0f9c-ec9e-48d7-bfc8-5aee62c25017/smll201804706-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201804706-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201804706-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a facile and low-cost surface solvation method has been successfully adopted to synthesize Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticle. During the ethanol treatment step, our experimental and computational tools determined that alcohol molecules could spontaneously bind to hydroxyl-terminated Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> surface and then dehydrate with the surface hydroxyl group to form Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub> surface, yielding soft agglomerates. The electrochemical mechanism of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> was characterized by in situ XRD for the first time. When evaluated as the anode material for lithium-ion batteries, the obtained novel Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticle exhibits an excellent rate capability (a high reversible discharge capacity of 679 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), good cycling stability (78% of the capacity is retained after 500 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), as well as improved initial coulombic efficiency (around 80%). While tested as SIBs anode, a high reversible capacity of 382 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be delivered at the current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which still retained as 229 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles. Thus, the superior electrochemical performance of the obtained material suggests that the solvation treatment method is a feasible way to reduce the hard-agglomeration phenomenon of primary nanoparticles, improving the dispersibility and electrochemical performance of active materials. Furthermore, this strategy can be extended for the preparation of next-generation high-performance cathode and anode materials composed of primary nanoparticles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201804706-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201804706-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis</i>: All chemicals were used as received without further purification. The samples were synthesized through the simple and cost-effective hydrothermal process with subsequent calcination under inert atmosphere as follows: 0.808 g iron nitrate nonahydrate (FeNO<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O) was dispersed in 18 mL deionized (DI) water under magnetic stir, 0.234 g of ammonium vanadate (NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub>) was dissolved in DI water under 80 °C to obtain a clear yellow solution. After that, NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> solution was added into the FeNO<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O solution slowly and stirred for 1 h to form yellow suspension. With the addition of 2 mL ethylenediamine, the yellow suspension was gradually turned into reddish brown solution. After stirring for 6 h at room temperature, the solution was transformed into a Teflon autoclave and heated at 180 °C for 48 h. The obtained gray brown precipitate was collected, washed with DI water and ethanol for several times. After that, the precipitate was redispersed in ethanol for 24 h to exchange the surface water with ethanol, which is the critical step in this synthesis process. Finally, the resulting products were dried and sintered at 450 °C for 10 h in N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere to get the final dark product, which is the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticles. For comparison, the contrast sample was produced by the same procedure without the ethanol treatment, which is proved to be the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> dense microparticles.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterization</i>: The samples were characterized by XRD (Bruker, D8 Discover with nonmonochromated Cu Kα X-ray source), SEM with EDS mapping (JEOL JSM-7100F), TEM and high-resolution TEM (JEOL JEM-2100F), FTIR (Nicolet iS50), specific surface area analyzer (Micromeritics Tristar 3020 instrument at 77 K), particle size analysis (Malvern Mastersizer 2000), and zeta potential analysis (Malvern Zetasizer Nano ZS).</p>
         
         <p>To make the electrode for in situ XRD measurement, the 70% Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>-PMP was grounded with 20% acetylene black and 10% poly(tetrafluoroethylene) (PTFE) to get circular electrode disks. After that, the obtained electrode was assembled into a specific cell with an X-ray-transparent beryllium window. Then, the in situ XRD signals of electrode during the charge/discharge process were collected directly using the planar detector under still mode for every 2 min.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurement</i>: The working electrode was prepared by mixing active material, super P, and sodium alginate in a weight ratio of 7:2:1 by DI water, then casting the slurries on copper foils. After drying at 80 °C, the electrodes were punched into circular disks with an average active material mass loading around 1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, and further assembled into CR2016-type coin cell. For LIBs test, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbon (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1 by volume) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte was used. The Celgard 2500 membrane was employed as the separator, and Li foil served as reference/counter electrode. For SIBs test, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DME) with 5% FEC as the electrolyte was used. Whatman glass fiber membrane was used as the separator, and Na foil served as reference/counter electrode. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed on Neware battery testing system (CT-4008) within a voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V. While the in situ XRD test was operated from 2.5 to 0.27 V. CV tests were implemented on a CHI 760D electrochemical workstation between 0.01 and 2.5 V. AC electrochemical impedance spectra were carried out using CHI 760D electrochemical workstation in the frequency range of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz (cell voltage of 2.20 V). All of the tests were performed at room temperature.</p>
         
         <p><i>Calculation Method</i>: Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) software was applied to perform the spin-polarized DFT calculations with periodic boundary calculations.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0049_R_d58303638e1622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. B">49</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0050_R_d58303638e1622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Phys. Rev. B">50</a></span> The projector augmented plane wave (PAW) pseudopotential combined with the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof generalized gradient approximation (PBE-GGA) exchange-correlation functional were applied to describe the ionic cores.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0051_R_d58303638e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Phys. Rev. B">51</a>, <a href="#smll201804706-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0052_R_d58303638e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">52</a></span> The plane-wave expansion basis was set with a cutoff energy of 450 eV to operate the structural optimizations. The Brillouin zones were sampled with 3 × 3 × 3 Monkhorst–Pack grids for the bulk geometry optimization. The energy convergence tolerance was set to below 10<sup>−5</sup> eV per unit cell, and all atoms were relaxed until the residual force was less than 0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. Supercells with dimensions of 14.61 Å × 11.8684 Å × 24.4535 Å with OH termination were used to model the Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (−112) surface, where a vacuum region of 15 Å was used to eliminate interactions between the neighboring cells of slab models under periodic conditions. For all slab calculations, the Brillouin zones were sampled with 3 × 3 × 3 Monkhorst–Pack grids. The reaction free energy changes were calculated as Δ<i>G</i> = Δ<i>E</i> + Δ<i>E</i><sub>ZPE</sub> − <i>T</i>Δ<i>S</i>, where Δ<i>E</i>, Δ<i>E</i><sub>ZPE</sub>, and Δ<i>S</i> are electronic energy, zero-point energy, and entropy difference between products and reactants. The frequency analysis was performed by VASP to calculate the zero-point energies of absorbed molecules. The entropies and vibrational frequencies of molecules in the gas phase were taken from the NIST database.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0053_R_d58303638e1654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53">53</a></span> The structure visualization was performed with <i>VESTA</i> software package version 3.<span><a href="#smll201804706-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201804706-bib-0054_R_d58303638e1659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">54</a></span></p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201804706-sec-0050-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>

                        <span><a
